        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833"><meta property="og:title" content="Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/acs.nanolett.9b02833.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Low utilization of active metallic sodium (Na) and uncontrollable growth of Na dendrites remain significant challenges for high-performance Na metal batteries, which are limited to inefficient Na utilization (<1%) and shallow cycling conditions (0.25–1.0 mAh cm–2). In this work, a kind of Na metal anode with record-high utilization and long-term cycling stability is reported, using carbon-substrate-supported nitrogen-anchored zinc (Zn) single atoms as a current collector. Single Zn atom sites which&nbsp;serve as a strong “magnet” for Na ions,&nbsp;can guide the metallic Na uniform nucleation and free from dendrite-induced short circuit. The nucleation overpotential of our strategy is essentially zero, where most of the reported modified substrates were&nbsp;greatly exceed 20 mV. Specifically, the Na anodes exhibit a high Na stripping/plating Coulombic efficiency with 99.8% over 350 cycles and a stable voltage response with small voltage hysteresis after cycling 1000 h. The full cell exhibits high Na utilization up to 100% and superior long-term cycling stability for more than 1000 cycles with excellent capacity retention. In terms of lifetime and Na utilization, the Na metal anodes based on&nbsp;our strategy&nbsp;significantly outperforms the reported state-of-the-art Na metal anodes. Moreover, this affords new insights into the controllable Na nucleation behavior and high Na utilization and sheds fresh light on atomic level design of an electrode for Na metal anodes."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/acs.nanolett.9b02833.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da86b68c46b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da86b68c46b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'dd52b76d-0584-4f26-8b73-bd0b211e30b3'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da86b68c46b325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da86b68c46b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="90da86b68c46b325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mega High Utilization of Sodium Metal Anodes Enabled by Single Zinc Atom Sites", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

215123, China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Jun++Zhong">Jun Zhong</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img China</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>E-mail: <a href="mailto:c.yan@suda.edu.cn">c.yan@suda.edu.cn</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Chenglin++Yan">Chenglin Yan</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7827%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTingzhou%2BYang%252C%2BTao%2BQian%252C%2BYawen%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D19%26issueNum%3D11%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.9b02833%26title%3DMega%2BHigh%2BUtilization%2Bof%2BSodium%2BMetal%2BAnodes%2BEnabled%2Bby%2BSingle%2BZinc%2BAtom%2BSites%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7835%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2019" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Low utilization of active metallic sodium (Na) and uncontrollable growth of Na dendrites remain significant challenges for high-performance Na metal batteries, which are limited to inefficient Na utilization (&lt;1%) and shallow cycling conditions (0.25–1.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>). In this work, a kind of Na metal anode with record-high utilization and long-term cycling stability is reported, using carbon-substrate-supported nitrogen-anchored zinc (Zn) single atoms as a current collector. Single Zn atom sites which&nbsp;serve as a strong “magnet” for Na ions,&nbsp;can guide the metallic Na uniform nucleation and free from dendrite-induced short circuit. The nucleation overpotential of our strategy is essentially zero, where most of the reported modified substrates were&nbsp;greatly exceed 20 mV. Specifically, the Na anodes exhibit a high Na stripping/plating Coulombic efficiency with 99.8% over 350 cycles and a stable voltage response with small voltage hysteresis after cycling 1000 h. The full cell exhibits high Na utilization up to 100% and superior long-term cycling stability for more than 1000 cycles with excellent capacity retention. In terms of lifetime and Na utilization, the Na metal anodes based on&nbsp;our strategy&nbsp;significantly outperforms the reported state-of-the-art Na metal anodes. Moreover, this affords new insights into the controllable Na nucleation behavior and high Na utilization and sheds fresh light on atomic level design of an electrode for Na metal anodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7827%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTingzhou%2BYang%252C%2BTao%2BQian%252C%2BYawen%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D19%26issueNum%3D11%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.9b02833%26title%3DMega%2BHigh%2BUtilization%2Bof%2BSodium%2BMetal%2BAnodes%2BEnabled%2Bby%2BSingle%2BZinc%2BAtom%2BSites%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7835%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290743&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Deposition" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Deposition" class="article__tags__link">Deposition</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290797&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Metals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Metals" class="article__tags__link">Metals</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291832&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nucleation" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nucleation" class="article__tags__link">Nucleation</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Single+Zn+Atom&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Single%20Zn%20atom">Single Zn atom</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na+Metal+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na%20metal%20anode">Na metal anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Na+Utilization&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20Na%20utilization">high Na utilization</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Controllable+Na+Nucleation+Behavior&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_controllable%20Na%20nucleation%20behavior">controllable Na nucleation behavior</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Owing to the low cost and wide availability of sodium resources, the rapid development of battery technologies based on sodium ion batteries has emerged as one of the most promising alternatives to lithium ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 1 to 6">(1−6)</a> Notably, direct use of Na metal as an anode shows clear advantages, which possesses a low redox potential (−2.714 V vs the standard hydrogen electrode) and high theoretical specific energy (1166 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 7 to 10">(7−10)</a> Unfortunately, Na metal anodes have long been considered “unsafe” because of the uneven metallic deposition, unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), dendritic growth, and infinite relative volume change upon repeated plating/stripping cycles, which cause low reversibility, internal short circuit, safety hazards, and short cycle life. For realizing the commercialization of the rechargeable Na metal batteries, efforts must be made to resolve the serious issues of Na metal anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 11 to 16">(11−16)</a> Sharing a working principle similar to lithium (Li) metal batteries, extensive works, such as porous structures, electrolyte additives, artificial SEI layer, and spatial deposition control, have been made to inhibit dendrite formation and improve the electrochemical performance. Conductive Na metal can deposit anywhere on the skeleton of substrates with unpredictable shapes in principle, and there is no effective method to direct metal deposition with controllable position and morphology.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, Au, Ag, and Pt as heterogeneous seeds to control the uniform deposition of Li ions on the skeleton of substrates have been provided, which have a low barrier for Li plating. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> For commercialized production, it is very desirable to develop cost-effective materials for large-scale energy storage. Noble metal obviously could not fulfill the growing demand of practical applications for stable anodes, which astrict the revival of alkali metal batteries. In that sense, carbon and its derivatives are also promising candidates due to their naturally abundant resources on earth and they show a small but observable overpotential for Li nucleation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> However, Na metal exhibits poor wettability with carbon materials, so unfavorable Na deposition occurs on the substrates. At the maximum limit of metal dispersion, single atoms generate great interest owing to their potential for increased active sites, maximized atomic utilization efficiency, and closer structural resemblance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 20 to 30">(20−30)</a> Integrating single atoms on appropriate substrates can optimize the electronic property and significantly alter the reactivity, which provides an effective way to regulate Na deposition with controllable position and morphology.</div><div class="NLM_p">Herein, we propose the uniform dispersal of nitrogen-anchored Zn single atoms on the surface of carbon substrates (Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C) to maximize the utilization of Na and guide heterogeneous Na deposition spatially. This discovery suggests an effective solution that suppressing Na dendrite growth at the nucleating stages and seeding Na deposition is expected to lead to a safe and efficient Na metal battery and offer a mechanistic understanding of the working Na metal battery as well, which is systemically elucidated by material characterization, electrochemical analyses, and density functional theory (DFT) calculations. Specifically, the Coulombic efficiency of the Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode can maintain at 99.8% for 350 cycles, and the polarization of the Na metal anode is limited to 56 mV with an ultralong lifespan for more than 1000 h. Impressively, the electrode with minimum Na deposits performs functions similar to Na foil and indicates a good cycling stability, which further exhibits a high utilization of almost 100% with limited Na source. In terms of both lifetime and Na utilization, our strategy significantly outperforms the reported state-of-the-art Na metal anodes.</div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i2" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i2"> Results and Discussion</h3><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Design for the Controllable Na Plating Process</h4><div class="NLM_p">The original intention of our work for single atoms is to explore the nucleation pattern of Na and spontaneously regulate Na deposition with spatial and structural control. The proposed approach is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. Due to the concentrated ion flux and high nucleation barriers, the rough surface of bare Cu foil could lead to uneven charge distribution and rapid Na deposition. The newly formed Na deposits exacerbate the surface roughness of the substrate and promote the growth of Na dendrites and dead Na by the same mechanism (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). The carbon substrate shows lower nucleation barriers, which still hamper Na deposition on them, leading to locally enhanced Na ion flux and promoting the growth of dendritic structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). In sharp contrast, as proposed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, a uniform Na deposit layer without Na dendrites and mossy-like morphology is expected for Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C. Single Zn atom sites not only favor Na deposition with no nucleation barriers but also have an excellent affinity and strong interaction with Na ions during cycles, providing stable sodium-philic sites for Na atoms. Induced by the homodisperse single Zn atoms, Na ions are only selectively nucleated on the surface of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C, and Na deposits form with continued Na plating instead of Na dendrites. When the Na deposits are large enough, their boundaries are drawn together, and finally, a homogeneous Na deposit layer with a uniform cladding thickness is formed. Additionally and importantly, the Na ions stored in Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrodes act as a backup source to remit any irreversible Na loss and fully tap the potential for the high utilization of Na during cycles.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of Na plating behavior on (a) Cu foil, (b) carbon substrate, and (c) Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrodes. Serious Na dendrites form on the surface of bare Cu foil, which will pierce through the separator and form dead Na, causing the shorting between two electrodes. For carbon substrate, there are still some Na dendrites growing on the surface. In contrast, Na metal is selectively nucleated on uniform single Zn atom sites with a negligible nucleation overpotential, and the edge of the substrate remains smooth, free from pulverization or Na dendrites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> HAADF-STEM and XAFS Characterization of Single Zn Atom Sites</h4><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> illustrates the detailed morphology and synthesis of precursors and products. Initially, aligned and ordered zinc contained zeolitic imidazolate frameworks (Zn-ZIFs) were directly grown on the surface of carbon cloth by a commercialized solvent thermal method. The tetrahedral framework topologies of Zn-ZIFs reveal that metal centers are connected by four imidazolate linkers, and the imidazolate ligands provide a rich nitrogen source for the strong adsorption of single Zn atoms. After the growth, the substrate was uniformly covered by Zn-ZIFs with different polyhedral morphology of ca. 50–100 nm in diameter (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S1">Figure S1</a>). The single Zn atoms were obtained by pyrolysis and adsorption between Zn particles and imidazolate ligands. As seen from the transmission electron microscopy (TEM, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S2">Figure S2</a>) images, no obviously nanoparticles are observed. Furthermore, the homogeneous distributions of both O, N, and Zn on the substrates were confirmed by elemental mapping images of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C. High-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) was used to discern individual heavy atoms. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, the bright spots which correspond to single Zn atom sites are uniformly dispersed on the substrate, and all of the Zn species exist exclusively as isolated single atoms; neither nanoparticles nor clusters are detected. The crystal structure and chemical state between Zn-ZIFs and Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C were further investigated by X-ray diffraction (XRD, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S3">Figure S3</a>) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S4a,b">Figure S4a,b</a>). The peak representing Zn 2p can be observed distinctly in the spectra and XRD profiles of Zn-ZIFs between 10 and 20° match well with the corresponding patterns of ZIF-7, further demonstrating the efficient and phase pure synthesis of ZIF-7 with the substrates. After transferring to an atomic structure, the intensity of Zn 2p spectra is very weak, and no obvious foreign peaks are present in XPS survey spectra and XRD patterns of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C. These observations unambiguously indicate that Zn distributes mainly in the form of single Zn atom sites on the substrates.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Characterization of the morphology of single Zn atom sites. (a) TEM image and corresponding elemental mapping results for Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C. Scale bar, 50 nm. (b) HAADF-STEM image of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C. Single Zn atoms are highlighted in yellow circles. Scale bar, 2 nm. (c) Zn <i>K</i>-edge XANES spectra of the samples. (d) Zn <i>K</i>-edge Fourier transformed EXAFS spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To further confirm this point, the local electronic structure and its interaction with the substrates were further investigated by normalized X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectra and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra for Zn <i>K</i>-edges with the comparison to a standard ZnO foil. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c, the white-line intensity of XANES spectra reflects the oxidation state of the Zn species, and ZnO foils distinctly have a high intensity of oxidation state. Notably, the white-line intensity of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C is similar to ZnO foil, suggesting that the Zn became more positively charged. In other words, our strategy results in a higher percentage of single Zn atom sites and more electrons transfer from Zn to the substrates, further demonstrating that the electronic property can be effectively tuned by forming atomic scale Zn structures and the use of single atoms provides an effective way to control Na ion deposition. For the EXAFS spectra, the peaks at 1.58 and 2.92 Å are associated with the Zn–O/Zn–N and Zn–Zn peaks, where the Zn–Zn peak is observed by the curves of the ZnO foil (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). The Fourier transform of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C exhibits only one prominent peak at 1.58 Å from the Zn–N contribution. Compared with the ZnO foil, the significant intensity of Zn–N bonds indicates the presence of single Zn atom sites.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Spatially Controlled Deposition of Na Metal</h4><div class="NLM_p">Recently, some studies showed that the characteristics of the Na deposition have a great effect on Na nucleation behavior, and the magnitude of the nucleation overpotential for Na deposition on different substrates has become particularly relevant for the evolution of surface morphology and island growth. As observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the voltage profiles of bare Cu exhibit a typical Na plating curve with a sharp voltage dip and a following flat voltage plateau, where the voltage difference between the voltage platform and voltage dip is defined as the nucleation overpotential. The nucleation overpotential of metallic Na plating on carbon cloth (CC) and bare Cu foil is about 9 and 32 mV, respectively. Due to the large thermodynamic mismatch between substrates and Na, the high nucleation overpotentials are all used to overcome the heterogeneous nucleation barrier. In a sharp contrast, the case for Na metal deposition on Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C is quite different with 0 mV nucleation overpotential, which means that the use of ZnSA–N–C substrate can effectively eliminate nucleation barriers during the Na plating process. Na can form an alloy with Zn. If we look at the binary phase diagram between Na and Zn (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S5">Figure S5</a>), Zn can react with Na to form multiple NaZn<sub><i>x</i></sub> alloy phases. The voltage trace of the Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C shows a unique nucleation mechanism during the first Na plating process. During the plating process, the upper plateau between 0.02 and 0.2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> is attributed to the formation of multiple NaZn<sub><i>x</i></sub> alloy phases. When the cells are investigated at a high areal capacity, the upper plateau is still maintained at the incipient 0.2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> capacity (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S6">Figure S6</a>), further demonstrating that the multiple NaZn<i><sub>x</sub></i> alloy phases have been formed by a fixed plating process (with the capacity of 0.2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>). With the deepening of plating, the typical Na deposition process &lt;0 V can be observed until the end of the step, which is similar to the voltage profile of the bare Cu electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S7">Figure S7</a>). Moreover, we summarized the recently reported literature focusing on the nucleation overpotential for the Na metal anode. The Na metal nucleation overpotentials on carbon fiber, graphene film, carbon nanotube, and carbon layer are 95.5, 37, 25, and 20 mV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref13" aria-label="reference 9, 10 and 13">(9,10,13)</a> respectively, which further demonstrate that the introduction of single Zn atom sites can provide a novel pathway to improve the stability of Na metal anode and spontaneously guide Na deposition with controllable morphology and position. As validated by DFT calculations, single Zn atoms are homogeneously distributed on the surface of substrates and exhibit a relatively large binding energy of 0.34 eV with Na ion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). The strong binding energy with no nucleation overpotential is promising for spatially controlled deposition, which could serve as sodium-philic sites for Na ions and conveniently guide Na deposition, suppressing the growth of Na dendrite and realizing spatial control over Na nucleation. By contrast, the binding energy between the Na ion and carbon substrate is only 0.09 eV (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S8">Figure S8</a>), which is 3.8 times smaller than that of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Strong interaction and patterned deposition between single Zn atom sites and Na metal. (a) Voltage profiles of galvanostatic Na deposition on different substrates. (b) The nucleation overpotential of different substrates. (c) DFT calculations on the affinity between carbon and a single Zn atom site, to evaluate the strong interaction for Na ions. Na is expected to nucleate from single Zn atom sites. In situ optical microscopy observations of the Na plating process with (d) Na foil, (e) CC, and (f) Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To further illustrate the spatial control over Na deposition and efficient suppression of Na dendrite growth, <i>in situ</i> optical microscopy was used to display the dynamic process of Na plating. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, Na dendrites and dendritic structure start growing along the edges of bare Na foil and become increasingly obvious over time. The surface is full of dead Na and dendrites after only 30 min. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, some Na filaments still appear on the surface of CC substrate during the Na plating process. As sodium-philic sites, Na ions selectively nucleate on single Zn atom sites with a zero nucleation overpotential, and the surface of the interior skeleton is uniformly encapsulated by a Na deposit layer free from dead Na and dendrites (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f). Owing to the enlarged surface area and homogeneously distributed sodium-philic sites of our strategy, metallic Na can uniformly deposit onto the surface of interior 3D skeletons. The corresponding cross-section SEM views of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C after Na plating are investigated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S9">Figure S9</a>, which is in agreement with <i>in situ</i> optical microscopy measurement. We further investigated the controlled Na plating processes between Cu, CC, and Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C by SEM characterization. Na metal was plated on different substrates at a constant current density of 1.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with a high nucleation capacity from 3.0 to 10.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. The surface of bare Cu foil is full of dead Na particles and dendrites (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S10a">Figure S10a</a>). The growth of dendrites, even dead Na, can severely penetrate through the separator and cause an internal short circuit of the Na metal battery. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S10b">Figure S10b</a>, due to the serious lattice mismatch between hexagonal carbon and body-centered cubic structured sodium, Na is suffering from a relatively high nucleation barrier, which is required to nucleate on the carbon substrate surface. Na particles are preferentially deposited at the junctions of carbon substrates where the crossed fibers meet to minimize the nucleation barrier. With the increase of nucleation capacity, Na particles are inclined to grow into Na agglomerates and fill the surface with the isolated Na dendrites that emerge from the agglomerates. Contrary to this, single Zn atoms dispersed on the carbon substrate, as the homogeneously distributed nucleation sites to guide Na plating, first promote uniform nucleation. Then, Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C is covered when a certain amount of Na metal is plating, and the as-produced numerous Na nuclei during the initial nucleation stage directly guide the following Na plating process. Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C can accommodate effective Na metal to deposit inside its interspace. The surface of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C remains smooth even after 10.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> plating and no apparent pancake-like Na pieces are discovered, which suggested a uniform deposition/stripping process without recognizable Na dendrites (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S10c">Figure S10c</a>).</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Na Migration Properties of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C</h4><div class="NLM_p">Coulombic efficiency is one of the important indexes to confirm the electrochemical performance of Na metal batteries, which is calculated from the rate of Na stripping capacity to plating capacity. The cells using a Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anode as the working electrode and metallic Na foil as the counter electrode were assembled for the test. The single Zn atom sites lead to a significantly improved Coulombic efficiency and cycling stability of Na stripping/plating. The initial rising of Coulombic efficiency is ascribed to the activation reactions between Na metal and organic electrolyte, forming an interconnected solid-state Na conduction layer on the surface of the Na metal anode. When cycling at an elevated current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with the capacity limitation of 0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, the cells with a Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anode exhibited a stable and long cycle life over 350 cycles with an average Coulombic efficiency of 99.8% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="S11">S11</a>). When measured at a current density of 1.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with a capacity limitation of 0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, a high average Coulombic efficiency of 99.6% is achieved over 150 cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S12">Figure S12</a>). The excellent cycling stability with high Coulombic efficiency during the Na stripping/plating process for Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrodes is clearly benefiting from the high surface activity of atomic Zn, which presents a higher reactivity for adsorption of Na ions. And there is also a clear gain from the single Zn atoms that guide Na plating in the substrates homogeneously without dendrite formation. Furthermore, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows that the Coulombic efficiency of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrodes is about 99.4, 98.9, and 98.5% where the current density is varied from 2.0, 3.0, to 4.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, respectively. By contrast, the cell using a carbon electrode exhibited a fluctuating Coulombic efficiency for the initial 155 cycles, followed by a rapid decay, which could be attributed to the continual consumption of electrolyte and the growth of Na dendrites and dead Na during the stripping/plating processes.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Coulombic efficiency with an areal capacity of 0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> at a current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. (b) Comparison of the Coulombic efficiency of Na stripping/plating at a high current density of 2.0, 3.0, and 4.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. To evaluate the cycling stability and voltage hysteresis, 4 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> Na is first predeposited on the Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C into half cells. (c) Voltage profiles of symmetric cells with two identical electrodes at a current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, exhibiting excellent cyclic stability over 1000 h. (d) Voltage curves of different electrodes tested in symmetric cells at a high current density of 1.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To further evaluate the electrochemical behavior of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C as the anode during galvanostatic cycling, we initially assembled Na symmetric cells with two identical electrodes and performed electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests. Na–Cu, Na–CC, and Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anodes were synthesized by the electrodeposition of 4.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> of Na metal. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was first conducted prior to cell cycling to evaluate the initial solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation. As illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S13">Figure S13</a>, only one semicircle can be obtained at high frequency, attributing to the charge-transfer and SEI resistance. The interfacial resistances of Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C∥Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C (44.6 Ω) and Na–CC∥Na–CC (189.7 Ω) cells are much lower than that of the Na∥Na cell (1053.6 Ω). It was found that the resistance of the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C symmetric cell is significantly lower than that of any other substrates, which can be attributed to the successful suppression of dead Na. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c shows the long-term cycling profiles of the different symmetrical cells under the high current densities of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with the corresponding stripping/plating capacity of 0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. During the cycles, Na ions are transporting back and forth between the two Na metal electrodes. The voltage hysteresis gradually increases up to 436.7 mV for the Na∥Na cell and 314.1 mV for the Na–CC∥Na–CC cell and the cells ultimately fail with short cycling, corresponding to the occurrence of a dendrite-induced short circuit. In sharp contrast, the cell with Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrodes renders a tighter voltage hysteresis of 56 mV and a much more extended cycle life for more than 1000 h, which further indicates the uniform and reversible deposition of Na metal on the substrates. A similar behavior can be observed at a high current density of 1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> due to the seeded Na growth on single Zn atom sites (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="S14">S14</a>), which shows an extremely stable voltage profile over long-term cycling after 300 h of cycling with low voltage hysteresis. For a better proof of the practicability, we summarize the reported literature on the design of a stable Na metal anode, which is still in its infancy. Our strategy of introducing single Zn atom sites outperforms those previously reported on a modified current collector for a Na metal anode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Table S1">Table S1</a>, Supporting Information).</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Mega High Sodium Utilization by Na Full Cell</h4><div class="NLM_p">The utilization of Na is a key point to apply Na metal anodes for commercial Na ion batteries. More recently, metallic Na has been sought out as counter electrode in traditional Na-based batteries, but the capacity of Na anodes are usually excessed in comparsion of the capacity of cathodes. One of the challenges they must face in the optimization process is that the configurations of irreversible Na comsumption is related to electrode activation process and the formation of SEI. The Na full cells consisting of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (with a capacity of 0.2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>) and Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode were investigated to further demonstrate the advantages and high utilization of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). The Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anodes were prepared by the direct electrodeposition of Na with different predeposition capacities. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S15">Figure S15</a> shows the anterior five cyclic voltammetry cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and two sharp redox peaks are observed, attributed to the V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>2+</sup> redox couple and the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> redox couple. The galvanostatic cycling curves obtained for the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode (with a predeposition capacity of 1.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>) in a full cell are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, and the flat charge and discharge plateau are in agreement with cyclic voltammetry cycles and previous studies. The Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode exhibits a specific capacity of 95.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C and the observed cycling curves during subsequent cycles remain unaltered, which further demonstrate the high Na utilization and excellent reversibility of the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode made by our strategy.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Different nucleation capacities of Na are first predeposited on Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C for the preparation of a Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode. (a) Full cells are assembled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the cathode and Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C as the anode. (b) Voltage profiles of the charge/discharge process for the full cell. (c) Comparison for the cycle performance of different electrodes (1.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>) at 0.2 C. (d) Cycling performance of the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anode and Na–CC anode with high Na utilization of 40% (top) and 100% (bottom). (e) Long cycling stability with high Coulombic efficiency of almost 100% at 0.5 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="S16">S16</a> show a comparison of the cycling capabilities for different anodes with the limited 1.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> deposition capacities. Most of the Na deposits on the surface of Cu and CC in the form of Na dendrites and dead Na, and few can be effectively used during the cycles. The Na–Cu and Na–CC electrodes severely deteriorate the discharge capacity only after a few cycles, which is caused by the repeated formation of SEI layers, the continuous consumption of electrolytes, and the limited reversibility. The amount of Na ions on the whole Na metal battery system decreases rapidly when the capacities of electrodeposition are decreased from 0.5 to 0.2 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). This compels us to take full advantage of reversible deposition of Na metal during the cycles and suppress the formation of dead Na. At a current density of 0.2 C, the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C (with Na utilization of 40%) delivers a stable capacity of over 93.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 120 cycles, and a similar behavior can be observed by the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode with the Na utilization of 60% (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Figure S17">Figure S17</a>). Almost all of the results are restricted to inefficient Na utilization (&lt;1%) and shallow cycling conditions (&lt;200 cycles). Due to the superior Na electroactivity and directional deposition, the electrode with the record-breaking high Na utilization of 100% can provide performance benefits as the replacer for Na foil and display a high specific capacity and a remarkable cycling stability over 120 cycles. As a contrast, the full cell with corresponding Na–CC anodes exhibits a serious capacity decay after a few cycles due to the continuous Na loss during the repeated Na stripping/plating process, demonstrating low Na utilization of the CC substrates. To our best knowledge, it is the first time that such a high Na utilization of 100% was achieved via assembling the full cell with limited Na deposits, where most of the Na metal anodes take excessive Na resources to maintain their electrochemical performance. Furthermore, the long-term cycling performance involving 1000 cycles was carried out at a current density of 0.5 C for the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e, it can deliver a stable specific capacity of 79.6 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> and the Coulombic efficiency approaches 100%.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div class="NLM_p first last">In summary, we illustrate the potential of nitrogen-anchored single Zn atoms dispersed on the substrate as the basis of a metallic Na anode, which can control Na deposition over both position and morphology instead of simply delaying the growth of Na dendrites. Single Zn atom site modified electrodes can not only favor Na deposition with zero nucleation energy barrier but can also fully tap the potential for the high utilization of Na during cycles. The Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anode demonstrates a high Coulombic efficiency of 99.8% for at least 350 cycles and an extra stable long-term stripping/plating process over 1000 h with a tighter voltage hysteresis of 56 mV. What is more, the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anode with the minimized predeposition of Na (with a high Na utilization of 100%) exhibits excellent cycling stability of more than 1000 cycles with a high Coulombic efficiency of 100% in the full cells with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. Our work provides a brand new atomic modified route to solve the notorious issue with the Na metal anode and paves the way for the further development on advanced safe Na metal anodes for next-generation high energy density Na–O<sub>2</sub> and Na–S battery systems.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Materials and Methods</h3><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Synthesis of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C was synthesized via two steps including the synthesis of Zn-ZIFs and thermal treatment, and all of the chemicals were directly used after purchase without further purification. Typically, Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (1.0 g) was dissolved in methanol (40 mL); meanwhile, 2-methylimidazole (1.875 g) was also dissolved in methanol (40 mL). Subsequently, two solutions were mixed together and transferred to a Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave with a piece of carbon cloth (2 × 2 cm<sup>2</sup>). The materials were maintained at 120 °C for 6 h, and the Zn-ZIFs were thoroughly washed with ethanol several times. Then, the product was annealed in a quartz tube under the protection of the N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere (100 sccm) at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> with a high temperature of 900 °C for 1 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Synthesis of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></h4><div class="NLM_p last">Bulk V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was prepared by the thermal decomposition of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> at 450 °C for 6 h in air. Then, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (5.0 g), Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (2.82 g), V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (2.64 g), and 3-hydroxytyramine hydrochloride (0.4 g) were dissolved into deionized water (120 mL) and vigorously stirred for 4 h to obtain a mixture solution. This mixture was preheated at 350 °C for 4 h under the protection of the Ar atmosphere, reground, and refired at 700 °C for 8 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Electrochemical Measurement</h4><div class="NLM_p last">CR2032-type coin cells were employed in an argon-filled glovebox for repeated plating and stripping testing. The electrolyte was 1 M sodium perchlorate (NaClO<sub>4</sub>) in ethylene carbonate (EC)/dimethylcarbonate (DMC) (1:1 by volume) with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate. The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode was prepared by mixing active materials, polyvinylidene fluoride, and carbon black in a ratio of 8:1:1 with <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone as the solvent. The areal mass loading of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode was ∼1.8 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. After the activation process of one cycle at 0–1 V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, Na was first predeposited on the Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C into half cells with different nucleation capacities. For symmetric batteries, two identical Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anodes were reassembled with 80 μL of electrolyte and a Whatmen GF/A separator. The full cell assembled with Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C∥Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was tested on a CT2001A cell test instrument (Wuhan LAND Electronic Co., Ltd.) and a VMP3 (BioLogic Science instrument Co., Ltd.) electrochemical workstation, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Material Characterization</h4><div class="NLM_p last">Field emission scanning electron microscope imaging and field emission transmission electron microscope measurements were performed on a SU8010, Hitachi, Ltd., and a FEI Tecnai G220, FEI NanoPorts, Ltd. The microstructures and surface elemental analysis were analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (Escalab 250Xi, Thermo Fisher) and X-ray diffractometer (D8 Advance, Bruker). X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) data were collected on beamline 14W at the Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility (SSRF) and on beamline 17C1 at the Taiwan Light Source (TLS).</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Computational Details</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The Gaussian 09<sup>3</sup> suite of programs was used to simulate their covalent interaction, which is performed by utilizing the model and the density-functional theory (DFT). All of the atoms were simulated by using the 6-31G (d+p) basis set, and the gas-phase Gibbs free energy was used during all of the present discussions. The stationary points as local minima or transition states were characterized through analyzing the vibrational frequency within the same theoretical level. The binding energy (<i>E</i>) was defined as the energy difference between Na ion with different substrates (<i>E</i><sub>t</sub>) and the summation of corresponding substrates (<i>E</i><sub>1</sub>) and Na ion (<i>E</i><sub>2</sub>): <i>E</i> = <i>E</i><sub>1</sub> + <i>E</i><sub>2</sub> – <i>E</i><sub>t</sub>. Basis set superposition error was considered in the calculation of binding energy.</div></div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div
