Chem. Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>49</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">231</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">240</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DLuo%252C%2BW.%253B%2BShen%252C%2BF.%253B%2BBommier%252C%2BC.%253B%2BZhu%252C%2BH.%2BL.%253B%2BJi%252C%2BX.%2BL.%253B%2BHu%252C%2BL.%2BB.%2BAcc.%2BChem.%2BRes.%2B2016%252C%2B49%252C%2B231%25E2%2580%2593240%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.accounts.5b00482&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhj1ZaK8pxMcg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms11722"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Agrawral, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choudhury, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Archer, L. A.</span></span> <i>Nat. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">11722</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/ncomms11722</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DWei%252C%2BS.%253B%2BXu%252C%2BS.%253B%2BAgrawral%252C%2BA.%253B%2BChoudhury%252C%2BS.%253B%2BLu%252C%2BY.%253B%2BTu%252C%2BZ.%253B%2BMa%252C%2BL.%253B%2BArcher%252C%2BL.%2BA.%2BNat.%2BCommun.%2B2016%252C%2B7%252C%2B11722%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fncomms11722&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A stable room-temperature sodium-sulfur battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shuya; Xu, Shaomao; Agrawral, Akanksha; Choudhury, Snehashis; Lu, Yingying; Tu, Zhengyuan; Ma, Lin; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-energy rechargeable batteries based on earth-abundant materials are important for mobile and stationary storage technologies.  Rechargeable sodium-sulfur batteries able to operate stably at room temp. are among the most sought-after platforms because such cells take advantage of a two-electron-redox process to achieve high storage capacity from inexpensive electrode materials.  Here we report a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery that uses a microporous carbon-sulfur composite cathode, and a liq. carbonate electrolyte contg. the ionic liq. 1-methyl-3-propylimidazolium-chlorate tethered to SiO2 nanoparticles.  We show that these cells can cycle stably at a rate of 0.5 C (1 C=1675, mAh g-1) with 600 mAh g-1 reversible capacity and nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency.  By means of spectroscopic and electrochem. anal., we find that the particles form a sodium-ion conductive film on the anode, which stabilizes deposition of sodium.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwo96Ash7WNbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljcMPwB5REpDQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitVCrsLjE&amp;md5=d938be66a2a0948e3d2f589b803dbf76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201503221"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, T.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li and Na metal batteries are promising next-generation energy storage devices due to their ultrahigh energy densities.  The high reactivity of alkali metal toward org. solvents and salts results in side reactions, which further lead to undesirable electrolyte depletion, cell failure, and evolution of flammable gas.  Herein, 1st-principles calcns. and in situ optical microscopy are used to study the mechanism of org. electrolyte decompn. and gas evolution on a Na metal anode.  Once complexed with Na ions, solvent mols. show a reduced LUMO, which facilitates the electrolyte decompn. and gas evolution.  Such a general mechanism is also applicable to Li and other metal anodes.  The authors uncover the crit. role of ion-solvent complexation for the stability of alkali metal anodes, reveal the mechanism of electrolyte gassing, and provide a mechanistic guidance to electrolyte and Li/Na anode design for safe rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqh1CIwmQIRgLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljcMPwB5REpDQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvFGitLrN&amp;md5=11620516ddc5dcb01cbc13d5a9568319</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702764"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chi, S.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been pursued as a more cost-effective and more sustainable alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), but these advantages come at the expense of energy d.  In this work, we demonstrate that the challenge of energy d. for sodium chemistries can be overcome through an anode-free architecture enabled by the use of a nanocarbon nucleation layer formed on Al current collectors.  Electrochem. studies show this configuration to provide highly stable and efficient plating and stripping of sodium metal over a range of currents up to 4 mA/cm2, sodium loading up to 12 mAh/cm2, and with long-term durability exceeding 1000 cycles at a current of 0.5 mA/cm2.                 10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b03071</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DWang%252C%2BH.%253B%2BWang%252C%2BC.%2BL.%253B%2BMatios%252C%2BE.%253B%2BLi%252C%2BW.%2BY.%2BNano%2BLett.%2B2017%252C%2B17%252C%2B6808%25E2%2580%25936815%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.7b03071&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Critical Role of Ultrathin Graphene Films with Tunable Thickness in Enabling Highly Stable Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Huan; Wang, Chuanlong; Matios, Edward; Li, Weiyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6808-6815</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na metal showed great promise as an anode material for the next-generation energy storage systems because of its high theor. capacity, low cost, and high earth abundance.  However, the extremely high reactivity of Na metal with org. electrolyte gives unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and growth of Na dendrites upon repeated electrochem. stripping/plating, causing poor cycling performance, and serious safety issues.  Herein, the authors present highly stable and dendrite-free Na metal anodes over a wide current range and long-term cycling via directly applying free-standing graphene films with tunable thickness on Na metal surface.  The authors systematically study the dependence of Na anode stability on the thickness of the graphene film at different current densities and capacities.  Only a few nanometer (∼2-3 nm) differences in the graphene thickness can have decisive influence on the stability and rate capability of Na anodes.  To achieve the optimal performance, the thickness of the graphene film covered on Na surface needs to be meticulously selected based on the applied c.d.  With a multilayer graphene film (∼5 nm in thickness) as a protective layer, stable Na cycling behavior was 1st achieved in carbonate electrolyte without any additives over 100 cycles at a c.d. ≤2 mA/cm2 with a high capacity of 3 mA h/cm2.  The authors believe the work could be a viable route toward high-energy Na battery systems, and can provide valuable insights into the Li batteries as well.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKQT_0ZsM4qrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1urH4U3HYaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1Kns7jL&amp;md5=616174934b1681e7a333506cde1b6a1e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE01373J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adair, K. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X. L.</span></span> <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2673</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2695</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C8EE01373J</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DZhao%252C%2BY.%253B%2BAdair%252C%2BK.%2BR.%253B%2BSun%252C%2BX.%2BL.%2BEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%2B2018%252C%2B11%252C%2B2673%25E2%2580%25932695%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC8EE01373J&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent developments and insights into the understanding of Na metal anodes for Na-metal batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Yang; Adair, Keegan R.; Sun, Xueliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2673-2695</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable Na-based battery systems, including Na-ion batteries, room temp. Na-S, Na-O2, Na-CO2, and all-solid-state Na metal batteries, have attracted significant attention due to the high energy d., abundance, low cost, and suitable redox potential of Na metal.  However, the Na metal anode faces several challenges, including: (1) the formation of Na dendrites and short circuiting; (2) low Coulombic efficiency (CE) and poor cycling performance; and (3) an infinite vol. change due to its hostless nature.  Furthermore, the issues assocd. with Na metal anodes have also been noticed in practical Na metal batteries (NMBs).  In recent years, the importance of the Na metal anode has been highlighted and many studies have provided potential solns. to address the issues of its use.  This review article focuses on the recent developments of Na metal anodes, including insight into the fundamental understanding of its electrochem. processes, novel characterization methods, approaches for protecting the anode and future perspectives.  Our review will accelerate further improvement in the characterization and application of Na metal anodes for next-generation NMB systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqGCfkfNcLuSbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1urH4U3HYaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhsVSqs7nP&amp;md5=d33a49fc969a36f1b793f8ac12951c58</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201606663"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goncharova, L.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Selective deposition and stable encapsulation of lithium through heterogeneous seeded growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yan, Kai; Lu, Zhenda; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Xiong, Feng; Hsu, Po-Chun; Li, Yuzhang; Zhao, Jie; Chu, Steven; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal is an attractive anode material for rechargeable batteries, owing to its high theor. specific capacity of 3,860 mAh g-1.  Despite extensive research efforts, there are still many fundamental challenges in using lithium metal in lithium-ion batteries.  Most notably, crit. information such as its nucleation and growth behavior remains elusive.  Here we explore the nucleation pattern of lithium on various metal substrates and unravel a substrate-dependent growth phenomenon that enables selective deposition of lithium metal.  With the aid of binary phase diagrams, we find that no nucleation barriers are present for metals exhibiting a definite soly. in lithium, whereas appreciable nucleation barriers exist for metals with negligible soly.  We thereafter design a nanocapsule structure for lithium metal anodes consisting of hollow carbon spheres with nanoparticle seeds inside.  During deposition, the lithium metal is found to predominantly grow inside the hollow carbon spheres.  Such selective deposition and stable encapsulation of lithium metal eliminate dendrite formation and enable improved cycling, even in corrosive alkyl carbonate electrolytes, with 98% coulombic efficiency for more than 300 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPXEqfqfc41LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1urH4U3HYaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVekur4%253D&amp;md5=1efe7249d8c35f8ceb20beb6dd70f69c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201702714"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, C.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Design principles for electrolytes and interfaces for stable lithium-metal batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tikekar, Mukul D.; Choudhury, Snehashis; Tu, Zhengyuan; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">16114</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The future of electrochem. energy storage hinges on the advancement of science and technol. that enables rechargeable batteries that utilize reactive metals as anodes.  With specific capacity more than ten times that of the LiC6 anode used in present-day lithium-ion batteries, cells based on Li-metal anodes are of particular interest.  Effective strategies for stabilizing the anode in such cells are now understood to be a requirement for progress on exceptional storage technologies, including Li-S and Li-O2 batteries.  Multiple challenges-parasitic reactions of Li-metal with liq. electrolytes, unstable and dendritic electrodeposition, and dendrite-induced short circuits-derailed early efforts to commercialize such lithium-metal batteries.  Here we consider approaches for rationally designing electrolytes and Li-metal/electrolyte interfaces for stable, dendrite-free operation of lithium-metal batteries.  On the basis of fundamental understanding of the failure modes of reactive metal anodes, we discuss the key variables that govern the stability of electrodeposition at the Li anode and propose a universal framework for designing stable electrolytes and interfaces for lithium-metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo1Ua24gk-RaLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1urH4U3HYaA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVersL8%253D&amp;md5=3627028eaacfbf47fd2a8691cfddcef1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms13638"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stambula, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Banis, M.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Platinum single-atom and cluster catalysis of the hydrogen evolution reaction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cheng, Niancai; Stambula, Samantha; Wang, Da; Banis, Mohammad Norouzi; Liu, Jian; Riese, Adam; Xiao, Biwei; Li, Ruying; Sham, Tsun-Kong; Liu, Li-Min; Botton, Gianluigi A.; Sun, Xueliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Platinum-based catalysts have been considered the most effective electrocatalysts for the hydrogen evolution reaction in water splitting.  However, platinum utilization in these electrocatalysts is extremely low, as the active sites are only located on the surface of the catalyst particles.  Downsizing catalyst nanoparticles to single atoms is highly desirable to maximize their efficiency by utilizing nearly all platinum atoms.  Here we report on a practical synthesis method to produce isolated single platinum atoms and clusters using the at. layer deposition technique.  The single platinum atom catalysts are investigated for the hydrogen evolution reaction, where they exhibit significantly enhanced catalytic activity (up to 37 times) and high stability in comparison with the state-of-the-art com. platinum/carbon catalysts.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The recent availability of shale gas has led to a renewed interest in C-H bond activation as the first step towards the synthesis of fuels and fine chems.  Heterogeneous catalysts based on Ni and Pt can perform this chem., but deactivate easily due to coke formation.  Cu-based catalysts are not practical due to high C-H activation barriers, but their weaker binding to adsorbates offers resilience to coking.  Using Pt/Cu single-atom alloys (SAAs), we examine C-H activation in a no. of systems including Me groups, methane and butane using a combination of simulations, surface science and catalysis studies.  We find that Pt/Cu SAAs activate C-H bonds more efficiently than Cu, are stable for days under realistic operating conditions, and avoid the problem of coking typically encountered with Pt.  Pt/Cu SAAs therefore offer a new approach to coke-resistant C-H activation chem., with the added economic benefit that the precious metal is dild. at the at. limit.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGppx4I2YTJ6s7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljYc1Cnx-S8Bg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXlt1yrsg%253D%253D&amp;md5=db8b4b1c1f6fe319a32be051dfb64e66</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201806936"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitchell, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vorobyeva, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pérez-Ramírez, J.</span></span> <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. redn. reaction of carbon dioxide (CO2RR) to carbon monoxide (CO) is the basis for the further synthesis of more complex carbon-based fuels or attractive feedstock.  Single-atom catalysts have unique electronic and geometric structures with respect to their bulk counterparts, thus exhibiting unexpected catalytic activities.  A nitrogen-anchored Zn single-atom catalyst is presented for CO formation from CO2RR with high catalytic activity (onset overpotential down to 24 mV), high selectivity (Faradaic efficiency for CO (FEco) up to 95 % at -0.43 V), remarkable durability (&gt;75 h without decay of FEco), and large turnover frequency (TOF, up to 9969 h-1).  Further exptl. and DFT results indicate that the four-nitrogen-anchored Zn single atom (Zn-N4) is the main active site for CO2RR with low free energy barrier for the formation of *COOH as the rate-limiting step.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoVT1IPyFB4IrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgI0V14j_GOPw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhsFOqsbnM&amp;md5=067e0e7143034b7ac20ab7683169ef64</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1021/ar300361m"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X. F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, B. T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.</span></span> <i>Acc. Chem.                 10.1021/ar300361m</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DYang%252C%2BX.%2BF.%253B%2BWang%252C%2BA.%253B%2BQiao%252C%2BB.%2BT.%253B%2BLi%252C%2BJ.%253B%2BLiu%252C%2BJ.%2BY.%253B%2BZhang%252C%2BT.%2BAcc.%2BChem.%2BRes.%2B2013%252C%2B46%252C%2B1740%25E2%2580%25931748%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Far300361m&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Single-Atom Catalysts: A New Frontier in Heterogeneous Catalysis</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Xiao-Feng; Wang, Aiqin; Qiao, Botao; Li, Jun; Liu, Jingyue; Zhang, Tao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1740-1748</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review; supported metal nanostructures are the most widely used type of heterogeneous catalyst in industrial processes.  The size of metal particles is a key factor in detg. the performance of such catalysts.  In particular, because low-coordinated metal atoms often function as the catalytically active sites, the specific activity per metal atom usually increases with decreasing size of the metal particles.  However, the surface free energy of metals increases significantly with decreasing particle size, promoting aggregation of small clusters.  Using an appropriate support material that strongly interacts with the metal species prevents this aggregation, creating stable, finely dispersed metal clusters with a high catalytic activity, an approach industry has used for a long time.  Nevertheless, practical supported metal catalysts are inhomogeneous and usually consist of a mixt. of sizes from nanoparticles to subnanometer clusters.  Such heterogeneity not only reduces the metal atom efficiency but also frequently leads to undesired side reactions.  It also makes it extremely difficult, if not impossible, to uniquely identify and control the active sites of interest.  The ultimate small-size limit for metal particles is the single-atom catalyst (SAC), which contains isolated metal atoms singly dispersed on supports.  SACs maximize the efficiency of metal atom use, which is particularly important for supported noble metal catalysts.  Moreover, with well-defined and uniform single-atom dispersion, SACs offer great potential for achieving high activity and selectivity.  In this Account, we highlight recent advances in prepn., characterization, and catalytic performance of SACs, with a focus on single atoms anchored to metal oxides, metal surfaces, and graphene.  We discuss exptl. and theor. studies for a variety of reactions, including oxidn., water gas shift, and hydrogenation.  We describe advances in understanding the spatial arrangements and electronic properties of single atoms, as well as their interactions with the support.  Single metal atoms on support surfaces provide a unique opportunity to tune active sites and optimize the activity, selectivity, and stability of heterogeneous catalysts, offering the potential for applications in a variety of industrial chem. reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrV0YMIdLZFdLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgI0V14j_GOPw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVChu73O&amp;md5=2044a224f72851911ecc960a90c6448d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1126/science.aaf7720"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Perry, I. B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchwald, S. L.</span></span> <i>Science</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>353</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">144</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">150</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Copper-catalyzed asymmetric addition of olefin-derived nucleophiles to ketones</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Yang; Perry, Ian B.; Lu, Gang; Liu, Peng; Buchwald, Stephen L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">353</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6295</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">144-150</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Enantioenriched alcs. found in an array of bioactive natural products and pharmaceutical agents are often synthesized by asym. nucleophilic addn. to carbonyls.  However, this approach generally shows limited functional-group compatibility, requiring the use of preformed organometallic reagents in conjunction with a stoichiometric or substoichiometric amt. of chiral controller to deliver optically active alcs.  Herein, the authors report a copper-catalyzed strategy for the stereoselective nucleophilic addn. of propargylic and other alkyl groups to ketones, using easily accessible (poly)unsatd. hydrocarbons as latent carbanion equiv.  This method features the catalytic generation of highly enantioenriched organocopper intermediates and their subsequent diastereoselective addn. to ketones, allowing for the effective construction of highly substituted stereochem. dyads with excellent stereocontrol.  Moreover, this process is general, scalable, and occurs at ambient temp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGobgNpHZnnpBbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgI0V14j_GOPw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFSisrvF&amp;md5=a6d1d9988551639565a2b340937835bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.8b00752"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Single-Atom Au/NiFe Layered Double Hydroxide Electrocatalyst: Probing the Origin of Activity for Oxygen Evolution Reaction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Jingfang; Liu, Jieyu; Xi, Lifei; Yu, Yifu; Chen, Ning; Sun, Shuhui; Wang, Weichao; Lange, Kathrin M.; Zhang, Bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">140</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3876-3879</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A fundamental understanding of the origin of oxygen evolution reaction (OER) activity of transition-metal-based electrocatalysts, esp. for single precious metal atoms supported on layered double hydroxides (LDHs), is highly required for the design of efficient electrocatalysts toward further energy conversion technologies.  Here, we aim toward single-atom Au supported on NiFe LDH (sAu/NiFe LDH) to clarify the activity origin of LDHs system and a 6-fold OER activity enhancement by 0.4 wt % sAu decoration.  Combining with theor. calcns., the active behavior of NiFe LDH results from the in situ generated NiFe oxyhydroxide from LDH during the OER process.  With the presence of sAu, sAu/NiFe LDH possesses an overpotential of 0.21 V in contrast to the calcd. result (0.18 V).  We ascribe the excellent OER activity of sAu/NiFe LDH to the charge redistribution of active Fe as well as its surrounding atoms causing by the neighboring sAu on NiFe oxyhydroxide stabilized by interfacial CO32- and H2O interfacing with LDH.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpKVonaZ0eMX7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgI0V14j_GOPw"> &gt;&gt; class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig1"><img class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of Na plating behavior on (a) Cu foil, (b) carbon substrate, and (c) Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrodes. Serious Na dendrites form on the surface of bare Cu foil, which will pierce through the separator and form dead Na, causing the shorting between two electrodes. For carbon substrate, there are still some Na dendrites growing on the surface. In contrast, Na metal is selectively nucleated on uniform single Zn atom sites with a negligible nucleation overpotential, and the edge of the substrate remains smooth, free from pulverization or Na dendrites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Characterization of the morphology of single Zn atom sites. (a) TEM image and corresponding elemental mapping results for Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C. Scale bar, 50 nm. (b) HAADF-STEM image of Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C. Single Zn atoms are highlighted in yellow circles. Scale bar, 2 nm. (c) Zn <i>K</i>-edge XANES spectra of the samples. (d) Zn <i>K</i>-edge Fourier transformed EXAFS spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Strong interaction and patterned deposition between single Zn atom sites and Na metal. (a) Voltage profiles of galvanostatic Na deposition on different substrates. (b) The nucleation overpotential of different substrates. (c) DFT calculations on the affinity between carbon and a single Zn atom site, to evaluate the strong interaction for Na ions. Na is expected to nucleate from single Zn atom sites. In situ optical microscopy observations of the Na plating process with (d) Na foil, (e) CC, and (f) Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Coulombic efficiency with an areal capacity of 0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> at a current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. (b) Comparison of the Coulombic efficiency of Na stripping/plating at a high current density of 2.0, 3.0, and 4.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. To evaluate the cycling stability and voltage hysteresis, 4 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> Na is first predeposited on the Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C into half cells. (c) Voltage profiles of symmetric cells with two identical electrodes at a current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, exhibiting excellent cyclic stability over 1000 h. (d) Voltage curves of different electrodes tested in symmetric cells at a high current density of 1.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/medium/nl9b02833_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0005.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Different nucleation capacities of Na are first predeposited on Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C for the preparation of a Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C electrode. (a) Full cells are assembled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the cathode and Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C as the anode. (b) Voltage profiles of the charge/discharge process for the full cell. (c) Comparison for the cycle performance of different electrodes (1.0 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>) at 0.2 C. (d) Cycling performance of the Na–Zn<sub>SA</sub>–N–C anode and Na–CC anode with high Na utilization of 40% (top) and 100% (bottom). (e) Long cycling stability with high Coulombic efficiency of almost 100% at 0.5 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833/asset/images/large/nl9b02833_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b02833&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></li><li id="pane-m2" aria-labelledby="pane-m2" class="tab__pane tab__pane-references"><div class="refs-header-label"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i17">References</h2></div></div></div><hr></div><p class="rightTab-references-count">

                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljWioyitqpA0w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms11722"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Agrawral, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choudhury, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Archer, L.                 10.1038/ncomms11722</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A stable room-temperature sodium-sulfur battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shuya; Xu, Shaomao; Agrawral, Akanksha; Choudhury, Snehashis; Lu, Yingying; Tu, Zhengyuan; Ma, Lin; Archer, Lynden A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11722</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-energy rechargeable batteries based on earth-abundant materials are important for mobile and stationary storage technologies.  Rechargeable sodium-sulfur batteries able to operate stably at room temp. are among the most sought-after platforms because such cells take advantage of a two-electron-redox process to achieve high storage capacity from inexpensive electrode materials.  Here we report a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery that uses a microporous carbon-sulfur composite cathode, and a liq. carbonate electrolyte contg. the ionic liq. 1-methyl-3-propylimidazolium-chlorate tethered to SiO2 nanoparticles.  We show that these cells can cycle stably at a rate of 0.5 C (1 C=1675, mAh g-1) with 600 mAh g-1 reversible capacity and nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency.  By means of spectroscopic and electrochem. anal., we find that the particles form a sodium-ion conductive film on the anode, which stabilizes deposition of sodium.  We also find that sulfur remains interred in the carbon pores and undergo solid-state electrochem. reactions with sodium ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdxq-Lo84ftbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljWioyitqpA0w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xpslymsb4%253D&amp;md5=41798f2b5b34a2e7fda7224a8c7d4100</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201402104"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">81</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">102</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4EE03192J</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">81-102</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwo96Ash7WNbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljWioyitqpA0w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitVCrsLjE&amp;md5=d938be66a2a0948e3d2f589b803dbf76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201503221"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, T. Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel concept for synthesizing N-doped carbon sheets (NDCS) is presented which uses okara as the source of both carbon and nitrogen, yielding a higher content of nitrogen (about 9.89 at%) than previous methods.                         rightTab-cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Critical Role of Ultrathin Graphene Films with Tunable Thickness in Enabling Highly Stable Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Huan; Wang, Chuanlong; Matios, Edward; Li, Weiyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6808-6815</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na metal showed great promise as an anode material for the next-generation energy storage systems because of its high theor. capacity, low cost, and high earth abundance.  However, the extremely high reactivity of Na metal with org. electrolyte gives unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and growth of Na dendrites upon repeated electrochem. stripping/plating, causing poor cycling performance, and serious safety issues.  Herein, the authors present highly stable and dendrite-free Na metal anodes over a wide current range and long-term cycling via directly applying free-standing graphene films with tunable thickness on Na metal surface.  The authors systematically study the dependence of Na anode stability on the thickness of the graphene film at different current densities and capacities.  Only a few nanometer (∼2-3 nm) differences in the graphene thickness can have decisive influence on the stability and rate capability of Na anodes.  To achieve the optimal performance, the thickness of the graphene film covered on Na surface needs to be meticulously selected based on the applied c.d.  With a multilayer graphene film (∼5 nm in thickness) as a protective layer, stable Na cycling behavior was 1st achieved in carbonate electrolyte without any additives over 100 cycles at a c.d. ≤2 mA/cm2 with a high capacity of 3 mA h/cm2.  The authors believe the work could be a viable route toward high-energy Na battery systems, and can provide valuable insights into the Li batteries as well.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKQT_0ZsM4qrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhsdFL5g0qLlw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1Kns7jL&amp;md5=616174934b1681e7a333506cde1b6a1e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE01373J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adair, K. However, the Na metal anode faces several challenges, including: (1) the formation of Na dendrites and short circuiting; (2) low Coulombic efficiency (CE) and poor cycling performance; and (3) an infinite vol. change due to its hostless nature. This review article focuses on the recent developments of Na metal anodes, including insight into the fundamental understanding of its electrochem. processes, novel characterization methods, approaches for protecting the anode and future perspectives.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium metal is an attractive anode material for rechargeable batteries, owing to its high theor. specific capacity of 3,860 mAh g-1.  Despite extensive research efforts, there are still many fundamental challenges in using lithium metal in lithium-ion batteries.  Most notably, crit. information such as its nucleation and growth behavior remains elusive.  Here we explore the nucleation pattern of lithium on various metal substrates and unravel a substrate-dependent growth phenomenon that enables selective deposition of lithium metal.  With the aid of binary phase diagrams, we find that no nucleation barriers are present for metals exhibiting a definite soly. in lithium, whereas appreciable nucleation barriers exist for metals with negligible soly.  We thereafter design a nanocapsule structure for lithium metal anodes consisting of hollow carbon spheres with nanoparticle seeds inside.  During deposition, the lithium metal is found to predominantly grow inside the hollow carbon spheres.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The future of electrochem. energy storage hinges on the advancement of science and technol. that enables rechargeable batteries that utilize reactive metals as anodes.  With specific capacity more than ten times that of the LiC6 anode used in present-day lithium-ion batteries, cells based on Li-metal anodes are of particular interest.  Effective strategies for stabilizing the anode in such cells are now understood to be a requirement for progress on exceptional storage technologies, including Li-S and Li-O2 batteries.  Multiple challenges-parasitic reactions of Li-metal with liq. electrolytes, unstable and dendritic electrodeposition, and dendrite-induced short circuits-derailed early efforts to commercialize such lithium-metal batteries.  Here we consider approaches for rationally designing electrolytes and Li-metal/electrolyte interfaces for stable, dendrite-free operation of lithium-metal batteries.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review; supported metal nanostructures are the most widely used type of heterogeneous catalyst in industrial processes.  The size of metal particles is a key factor in detg. the performance of such catalysts.  In particular, because low-coordinated metal atoms often function as the catalytically active sites, the specific activity per metal atom usually increases with decreasing size of the metal particles.  However, the surface free energy of metals increases significantly with decreasing particle size, promoting aggregation of small clusters.  Using an appropriate support material that strongly interacts with the metal species prevents this aggregation, creating stable, finely dispersed metal clusters with a high catalytic activity, an approach industry has used for a long time.  Nevertheless, practical supported metal catalysts are inhomogeneous and usually consist of a mixt. of sizes from nanoparticles to subnanometer clusters.  Such heterogeneity not only reduces the metal atom efficiency but also frequently leads to undesired side reactions.  It also makes it extremely difficult, if not impossible, to uniquely identify and control the active sites of interest.  The ultimate small-size limit for metal particles is the single-atom catalyst (SAC), which contains isolated metal atoms singly dispersed on supports.  SACs maximize the efficiency of metal atom use, which is particularly important for supported noble metal catalysts.  Moreover, with well-defined and uniform single-atom dispersion, SACs offer great potential for achieving high activity and selectivity.  In this Account, we highlight recent advances in prepn., characterization, and catalytic performance of SACs, with a focus on single atoms anchored to metal oxides, metal surfaces, and graphene.  We discuss exptl. and theor. studies for a variety of reactions, including oxidn., water gas shift, and hydrogenation.  We describe advances in understanding the spatial arrangements and electronic properties of single atoms, as well as their interactions with the support.  Single metal atoms on support surfaces provide a unique opportunity to tune active sites and optimize the activity, selectivity, and stability of heterogeneous catalysts, offering the potential for applications in a variety of industrial chem. reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrV0YMIdLZFdLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh3cYVfzBf05g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVChu73O&amp;md5=2044a224f72851911ecc960a90c6448d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1126/science.aaf7720"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Perry, I.
